(وَ) الثالث: (زَوَالُ مَـا يَمْنَعُ وُصُولَ المَاءِ إِلى الجَسَدِ) لحرمة الحائل، (كَشَمْعٍ، وَشَحْمٍ)، قيـد بـه، لأن بقاء دسـومة الدهـن كالزيت لا تمنع، لعدم الحائل.
Its cause: making lawful what is not lawful except by it, and its worldly ruling is that, and its otherworldly ruling: reward in the Hereafter. Conditions of its obligation: sanity, puberty, Islam, ability to use sufficient water, existence of hadath, absence of menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and narrowness of time. Conditions of its validity are three. ... The intended meaning of the verse, and as for estimating the obligatory (wipe) as three fingers, it is inconceivable both by transmission and knowledge, so it is not acted upon even if authenticated. The place of wiping is above the ears. If one wipes over his hair, it suffices, unlike if his two braids are tied tightly on his head and he wipes over their tops, then it is not valid. Wiping linguistically: passing the hand over something, and technically: touching the limb with a wet hand, even after washing the limb, not wiping it. And with wetness taken from a limb, and the implement is intended only for conveying. If water or rain reaches him in the obligatory amount, it suffices. (Its cause) i.e., wudu, and likewise the cause of purification. The cause is that which leads to something without having an effect in it. (Making lawful) i.e., intending to do (what) i.e., something like prayer, touching the mushaf, and tawaf, which is (not lawful) i.e., it is not permitted to proceed with it (except by it) because it i.e., wudu, (and it is) i.e., the permissibility of proceeding with the act while in wudu (its worldly ruling). This is what is specific to this context, so he said: (And its otherworldly ruling: reward in the Hereafter) because it is not specific to wudu, rather it is the ruling of every act of worship. The conditions of its obligation are eight, i.e., its necessity. (Sanity) because there is no address without it, (and puberty) because the minor is not obligated, although the validity of his prayer depends on purification, because it is said that it is prayer, since it is from the address of situational law. (And Islam) because the disbeliever is not addressed by the branches of the Sharia. (And ability) of the responsible person (to use water) that is pure, because lacking it negates ability, and need for water negates it legally, (sufficient) for all limbs, so a small amount that is not sufficient once is like non-existence, because there is no benefit in using it. (And existence of hadath) because the one who performs wudu is not obligated to renew wudu for prayer. (And absence of menstruation and) absence (of postpartum bleeding) i.e., their cessation upon completion of the usual period. (And narrowness of time) because he is addressed, at the entrance of time broadly, so when time becomes narrow, the action becomes necessary due to the address becoming narrow then. Thus the scholars mentioned it, and some of them counted nine. And I have summarized it into one that includes all by restrictions, so I said: The condition of obligation is the ability of the responsible person to perform purification with water. End. (And condition of its validity), i.e., wudu, (three) and in reality they reduce to one thing: covering the skin with water that is purifying for it legally; because it cannot be purifying for it unless it is pure water while everything that contradicts it and prevents its reaching the body is absent, but it was expanded for ease of teaching. (So he said: The first of the conditions of validity: covering the skin with pure water) such that if the amount of a needle's head remains untouched by water, the wudu is not valid. (And) the second: (cessation of what contradicts it from menstruation and postpartum bleeding) upon completion of the usual period, (and) cessation (of hadath) during wudu because if urine appears from him, or blood flows etc., his wudu is not valid, and thus we previously stated that it is not permissible to begin wudu until one is certain of the cessation of urinary discharge. (And) the third: (removal of what prevents water from reaching the body) due to the prohibition of a barrier, (like wax and fat), restricted by that, because the remaining grease of oil like olive oil does not prevent, due to the absence of a barrier.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the cause and conditions of wudu from Imam al-Haskafi's Nur al-Idah. It begins by defining the cause (sabab) of wudu, which is 'making lawful what is not lawful except by it' – meaning prayer, touching the Quran, and tawaf. The worldly ruling of wudu is this permission; the otherworldly ruling is reward. The author then enumerates eight conditions for wudu to be obligatory: sanity, puberty, Islam, ability to use sufficient pure water, existence of hadath (ritual impurity), absence of menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and narrowness of time (i.e., the prayer time is about to end). Some scholars count nine, but the author summarizes them as 'ability of the responsible person to perform purification with water'. Next, the passage discusses the conditions of validity of wudu, which are three in number but reduce to one: covering the skin with pure water while ensuring that nothing contradicts this (such as ongoing hadath, menses, or postpartum bleeding) and that no barrier prevents water from reaching the skin. Examples of barriers are wax and fat; oils like olive oil do not form a barrier. The commentary also inserts a discussion about the wiping (masah) part of wudu: the obligatory amount cannot be three fingers (that view is rejected), and the place of wiping is above the ears. Wiping over hair suffices, but not over tightly bound braids. The definition of masah is given: 'linguistically, passing the hand over something; technically, touching the limb with a wet hand' – it is not wiping away wetness. Overall, this passage provides a comprehensive foundation for understanding when wudu becomes obligatory and what makes it valid.

Key Terms
السبب (as-sabab): That which leads to something without having an effect in it; here, wudu is the cause for making lawful acts that are otherwise unlawful.
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual impurity that prevents performing acts like prayer; existence of hadath is a condition for obligation of wudu.
الطهور (at-tahur): Water that is pure and purifying; it must be used for wudu.
المسح (al-masah): Technically, touching the limb with a wet hand (not wiping away wetness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the conditions for the validity of wudu?
A) Covering the skin with pure water
B) Cessation of menstruation and postpartum bleeding
C) Existence of hadath
D) Removal of barriers like wax and fat

Key Takeaway
Wudu becomes obligatory when eight conditions (sanity, puberty, Islam, ability, hadath, absence of menses/postpartum, narrow time) are met, and it is valid when three conditions (covering skin, cessation of contradictions, removal of barriers) are fulfilled.

Three Conditions for Validity of Wudu
Lists three conditions for wudu validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for wudu to be valid and apply them to identify whether a given wudu is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Wudu is invalid if even a needle tip remains dry or a barrier blocks water.

Wudu is the key to prayer, but its validity hangs on three precise conditions. This passage from Nur al-Idah lists them as a memorization anchor. Understanding these conditions helps you avoid common mistakes in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition
Read the phrase 'general covering of the skin with purifying water'. This means every part of the washed limbs must be touched by water. Even a spot the size of a needle tip left dry invalidates wudu.
This condition applies to the entire skin of the face, hands, head, and feet.
2
Identify the second condition
Read 'cessation of what contradicts it from menstruation, postpartum, and hadath'. This means that during wudu, any ongoing ritually impure state (like menstruation) or active hadath (like dripping urine) must have stopped. Wudu cannot begin while such things are occurring.
Cessation of menstruation/postpartum is confirmed by completion of one's usual period.
3
Identify the third condition
Read 'removal of what prevents water from reaching the body, like wax and fat'. Any solid barrier that blocks water must be removed. However, oily substances that do not form a solid layer are not barriers.
Examples: wax, grease, paint, plaster. Exceptions: natural skin oils.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Given a specific case, check each condition: Is the skin fully covered? Is any hadath stopped? Are there barriers? Only if all three are satisfied is wudu valid.
This analytical step is essential for practical fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عُمُومُ البَشَرةِ بالماءِ الطَّهُورِ، وانقطاع ما ينافيه من - ونفاس، وحَدَثٍ وزوالُ ما يمنع وصول الماء إلى الجسد، كشمع، وشحم فقال: الأول من شروط الصحة: (عُمُومُ البَشَرةِ بالماءِ الطَّهُورِ) حتى لو بقي مقدار رأس إبـــرة لم يصبه الماء، لم يصح الوضوء. (وَ) الثاني: ( انقطاعُ ما ينافيه منْ حَيْضِ، ونفاس) لتمــام الـعــادة، (وَ) انقطاع (حَدَثٍ) حال التوضؤ لأنه لو كان يظهر منه بول، أو يسيل منه دم ونحوه لا يصح وضوؤه، ولذا قدمنا أنه لا يجوز الشروع في الوضوء حتى يتيقن بزوال رشح البول. (وَ) الثالث: (زَوَالُ مَـا يَمْنَعُ وُصُولَ المَاءِ إِلى الجَسَدِ) لحرمة الحائل، (كَشَمْعٍ، وَشَحْمٍ)، قيـد بـه، لأن بقاء دسـومة الدهـن كالزيت لا تمنع، لعدم الحائل.
General covering of the skin with purifying water, and cessation of what contradicts it from menstruation, postpartum, and hadath, and removal of what prevents water from reaching the body, like wax and fat. He said: The first of the conditions of validity: (General covering of the skin with purifying water) such that if a needle tip's amount remains not reached by water, the wudu is not valid. (And) the second: (Cessation of what contradicts it from menstruation and postpartum) due to completion of the norm, (and) cessation of (hadath) during the state of performing wudu because if urine appears from him or blood flows or similar, his wudu is not valid. Therefore we previously stated that it is not permissible to begin wudu until one is certain of the cessation of urine seepage. (And) the third: (Removal of what prevents water from reaching the body) due to the prohibition of the barrier, (like wax and fat). He restricted it to these because the persistence of greasiness of oil like olive oil does not prevent, due to absence of a barrier.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage lists the three conditions for the validity of wudu according to the Hanafi school. The first condition is that purifying water must reach the entire skin of the washed body parts; even a small area left dry invalidates wudu. The second condition requires that anything contradicting wudu must have ceased: this means menstruation or postpartum bleeding must have stopped (signaled by completion of one's usual period), and any ongoing hadath (like dripping urine or blood) must be absent during the act of wudu. The third condition prohibits barriers such as wax or solid fat that block water from reaching the skin, though greasy substances like oil that do not form a solid barrier are excused. These conditions are essential for wudu to be valid.

Key Terms
طهور (tahur): Purifying water that is inherently pure and can remove ritual impurity.
بشرة (basharah): The skin of the body that must be washed in wudu.
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity, a legal state that nullifies wudu.
حائل (ha'il): A barrier preventing water from reaching the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a small area of skin, the size of a needle tip, remains dry during wudu?
A) Wudu is still valid if the area is small
B) Wudu is valid but makruh
C) Wudu is invalid
D) Wudu is valid and the dry spot can be overlooked

Key Takeaway
Three conditions must be met for wudu to be valid: full coverage of skin, cessation of contradictions, and removal of barriers.

Washing the Beard in Wudu: Thick vs. Thin
Explains obligatory washing of thick and thin beard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for washing a thick beard and a thin beard during wudu, and apply the principle of 'muqabilat al-muwajahah' to determine the obligation.

Core Idea
The thick beard substitutes for the skin; the thin beard requires water to reach the skin.

In wudu, washing the face is fard. But what about the beard? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on density: if the beard is so thick that the skin is hidden, the obligation transfers to its outer surface. If it is thin and the skin is visible, water must reach the skin. This ruling is based on the principle of direct confrontation (muqabilat al-muwajahah) and the ease or difficulty of washing. Let's see how the classical text explains it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish beard types
Identify whether the beard is thick (skin not visible) or thin (skin visible). This determines the obligation.
The same beard may be considered thick or thin relative to its density.
2
State the ruling for each
For a thick beard: fard to wash the outer surface. For a thin beard: fard to cause water to reach the skin.
The text uses 'yajib' (obligatory) and 'yaftarid' (fard).
3
Understand the rationale
The thick beard takes the place of the skin, so the obligation shifts to it. The thin beard does not block full confrontation (muqabilat al-muwajahah), and washing it is not difficult, so the skin must be wetted.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the principle of substitution and ease.
4
Recognize the historical dispute
Some early Hanafi opinions allowed washing only a portion (one-third or one-fourth) or wiping. These were abandoned because they contradicted the principle.
The mufta bihi is the full washing of the outer part.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a person's beard type, determine what must be washed and whether the wudu is valid if a dry area remains.
Any dry area in the required region invalidates that limb's washing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما لم يقدم الكلام على اللحية، ذكره، فقال: (يَجِبُ) يعني: يفترض (غسلُ ظاهر اللحية الكنة)، وهي التي لا تُرى بشرتها من تحتها: (في أصَحُ مَا يُفْتَى بِهِ من التصاحيح في حكمها، وإنما كان هذا أصح؛ لأنها قامت مقام البشرة، فتحول الفرض إليها، وما قيل غير ذلك من الاكتفاء بثلثها أو ربعها، أو مسح كلها، أو غيره، متروك؛ لأنهم رجعوا عما سوى هذا، لما قلناه.(وَيجَبُ) أي: يلزم، بمعنى: يفترض ( إِبْصَالُ المَاءِ إِلى بَشَرةِ اللّحْيَةِ الخَفِيفَةِ في المختار لبقاء المواجهة بها وعدم عسر غسلها وقيل: ويسقط لانعدام المواجهة الكاملة بالنبات.
Since he had not previously spoken about the beard, he mentioned it. He said: (It is obligatory) meaning it is a fard (to wash the outer part of the thick beard), which is that whose skin is not seen from beneath it: (in the most correct view that is given fatwa according to the correct opinions in its ruling. And this is considered the most correct because it takes the place of the skin, so the obligation transfers to it. And whatever else was said – like being satisfied with washing one-third or one-fourth, or wiping all of it, or other – is abandoned because they retracted from all besides this, due to what we said. (And it is obligatory) i.e., binding, meaning fard (to cause water to reach the skin of the thin beard in the chosen view due to the face still being confronted with it and no difficulty in washing it. And it was said: it is waived due to the absence of complete confrontation because of growth.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the rulings of wudu concerning the beard. It first discusses the thick beard where the skin is not visible: it is obligatory (fard) to wash its outer surface, which now takes the place of the skin. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa, opposing weaker views that only required washing a portion or wiping. Then it addresses the thin beard where the skin is visible: here water must reach the skin itself because the face is still confronted and there is no hardship. There is a weaker opinion that it is not required due to the growth obstructing complete confrontation, but the chosen view is obligation.

Key Terms
اللحية الكثة (al-lihyah al-kaththah): A thick beard that completely conceals the skin underneath.
اللحية الخفيفة (al-lihyah al-khafifah): A thin beard where the skin is visible through it.
مقابلة المواجهة (muqabilat al-muwajahah): The state of the face being directly exposed to water, used as a reason for obligatory washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wipe over the entire beard
B) Wash one-third of the beard
C) Wash the outer part of the beard
D) Wash the skin under the beard

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires washing the outer part of a thick beard and reaching the skin of a thin beard; this is based on the principle of direct confrontation (muqabilat al-muwajahah) and the ease or difficulty of washing.

What Is Not Obligatory to Wash: Hairs, Lips, Wounds
Lists parts not requiring water in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of body parts that are exempt from washing in wudu and state the Hanafi reasoning for each exemption.

Core Idea
Inner lips, inner eyes, hanging hair, and healed wound scabs are not wajib to wash due to being internal or causing harm.

The command to wash the face in wudu is clear, but not every part that appears to be on the face is included. The Hanafi school specifies several exceptions: hair that hangs beyond the natural face boundary, the inner part of the lips, the inner part of the eyes, and the interior of a healed wound. These exemptions prevent unnecessary hardship and align with the principle that only the external, apparent parts of the face are required to be washed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hanging hair exemption
Locate the part about 'mustarsil' hair: it is not obligatory to wash hair that hangs down beyond the circle of the face, because it is not originally part of the face.
This applies to long beard hair below the chin.
2
Identify the lips exemption
Note the inner part of the lips that is concealed when the mouth is closed normally. It is considered part of the mouth interior, not the face, so it is not washed.
The outer, visible part of the lips remains wajib.
3
Identify the inner eyes exemption
The inner part of the eyes (the conjunctiva) is not washed even in ghusl, because attempting to wash it would cause harm.
This applies to both wudu and ghusl.
4
Identify the wound scab exemption
A healed wound with a scab: the interior under the scab is not washed. However, if there is an opening for pus, that opening must be washed as it is an exit.
The scab acts as a barrier that is considered internal until it separates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَلا يجب إيصال الماء إلى المسترسل مــن الشعر عن دائرة الوَجْهِ، لأنه ليس منه أصالة، وليس بدلاً عنه. (وَلا) يجب إيصال الماء (و إلى مَا انْكَتَمَ مِنَ الشَّفتينِ عِنْدَ الانْضِمَامِ المعتاد، فإن المنضم تبع للفم، في الأصح، وما ظهر تبع للوجه، ولا باطن العينين ولو في الغسل، لخوف الضرر. ولا داخل قرحة برئت ولم ينفصل من قشرها سوى مخرج القيح لعدم خروجه عن حكم الباطن بهذا القدر، لأنه ضروري.
(And it is not obligatory to cause water to reach the hair that hangs down from the area of the face, because it is not originally part of it, and it is not a substitute for it. (And it is not) obligatory to cause water (to reach what is concealed of the lips when they are closed in the customary manner, for what is concealed is part of the mouth, in the most correct view, and what appears is part of the face. And not the inner part of the eyes, even in ghusl, due to fear of harm. And not the inside of a wound that has healed and whose scab has not yet separated, except for the exit of pus, because it does not cease to be ruled as internal to this extent, because it is necessary.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage clarifies areas that are not required to be washed during wudu (and even ghusl for eyes). First: hair that grows beyond the natural boundary of the face (e.g., long beard hair hanging below the chin) is not part of the face, so washing it is not obligatory. Second: the inner parts of the lips that become hidden when the mouth is normally closed are considered part of the interior (mouth), not the face, so they are not washed. The outer parts of lips are washed. Third: the inner part of the eyes is not washed, even in ghusl, because it would cause harm. Fourth: a healed wound whose scab remains attached: the scab itself is not washed because it is considered internal; however, if there is an opening for pus, that area must be washed as it is an exit. The principle is that what is inside the body is not subject to washing.

Key Terms
مسترسل (mustarsil): Hair that hangs down beyond the natural boundary of the face.
انكتم (inkatama): What becomes concealed or hidden when the lips are closed.
قرحة (qurhah): A wound that has healed and formed a scab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) If there is an exit for pus
D) Always

Key Takeaway
The exemptions in wudu are based on the principle of preventing harm and recognizing what is considered internal or external.

Barriers and Excused Impurities in Wudu: Fingers, Nails, and Dirt
Ruling on barriers and excused impurities in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify barriers that invalidate wudu and impurities that are excused, applying the Hanafi ruling on water reaching the skin.

Core Idea
Only actual barriers blocking water must be removed; naturally generated dirt and minor impurities are excused.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies what prevents the validity of wudu regarding water contact. It distinguishes between external barriers like closed fingers, long nails, or dough, which must be removed, and internal or minor impurities like dirt under nails or flea droppings, which are excused. Understanding these boundaries ensures your wudu is correct without unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligations
Recognize what the text declares obligatory: washing what is under a barrier after removing it. This is fard (obligatory) for the validity of wudu.
The word 'wajab' here means fard (obligatory) in Hanafi terminology.
2
Recognize the barriers
List the three specific barrier scenarios: (1) closed fingers preventing water between them, (2) long nails covering the fingertip, (3) external substances like dough, wax, or dried eye residue (ramas) that block water.
These must be removed or circumvented for wudu to be valid.
3
Understand the excused impurities
Analyze what the text says does NOT prevent: natural dirt under nails (daran) regardless of whether the person is rural or urban, and small impurities like flea or fly droppings.
The rationale: these are either generated from the body or too difficult to avoid completely.
4
Apply the principle
Derive the underlying principle: any substance that forms a physical seal preventing water contact requires removal; substances generated from the body or too small to form a seal are excused.
This principle is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on ease (yusr) in taharah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولو انْضَمِّتِ الأصابع)، بحيث لا يصل الماء إلى أثنائها، أو طَالَ الظُّفْرُ فَغَطّى الأَنْمُلَةَ ، فمنع وصول الماء إلى ما تحته، أوْ كَانَ فِيهِ)، يعني: المحل المفروض غسله (مَا ) ، أي شيء (يَمْنَعُ المَاءَ) أن يصل إلى الجسد (كَعَجِيْن) وشمع ورمص (۳) يبقى خارج العين بتغميضها، (وَجَبَ) يعني: افترض (غَسْلُ ما تحتَهُ) بعد إزالة المانع ( ولا يَمْنَعُ الدَّرَن)، أي: الوسخ في الأظفار سواء فيه القروي والمصري، في الأصح، فيصح الغسل معه لتولده من البدن (و) لا يمنع (خُرْءُ البراغيث، ونحوها) كونيــم الذباب
(And if the fingers are closed) such that water does not reach between them, or the nail has lengthened and covered the fingertip, preventing water from reaching what is under it, or there is on it (meaning the place obligated to be washed) something that prevents water from reaching the body, like dough, or wax, or dry matter (3) that remains outside the eye when it is closed, (it is obligatory) meaning fard (to wash what is under it) after removing the barrier. (And dirt) i.e., filth under the nails, whether the person is a villager or city-dweller, does not prevent, in the most correct view, so washing is valid with it because it is generated from the body. (And) it does not prevent (flea droppings and the like), i.e., fly droppings.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses specific cases where water might be blocked. First: if fingers are tightly closed, water must be made to reach between them; if that is not possible without separating them, the separation is required. Second: if a nail is long enough to cover the fingertip, it becomes a barrier and must be cut or water must be washed under it. Third: any substance that prevents water contact, like dough or wax, must be removed, and washing underneath is obligatory. Fourth: ordinary dirt under the nails, common in both rural and urban people, does not prevent the validity of wudu because it is naturally generated from the body. Similarly, flea or fly droppings that are small do not create a barrier. The key principle is that only actual barriers preventing water contact require removal; minor impurities that do not form a seal are excused.

Key Terms
الدرن (al-daran): Dirt or filth under the nails generated naturally from the body.
خرء البراغيث (khur' al-baraghith): Flea droppings, small impurities that do not create a barrier.
رمص (ramas): Dried matter that collects at the corner of the eye.
حاجز (hajiz): A physical barrier that prevents water from reaching the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wax on the finger
B) Flea droppings on the arm
C) Dough on the hand
D) Long nails covering the fingertip

Key Takeaway
Wudu is invalid only if a physical barrier prevents water from reaching the skin; minor impurities like natural dirt and insect droppings are excused.

Rulings on Washing the Beard and Face in Wudu
Rulings on washing the beard and face parts in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for thick and light beards in wudu and apply the Hanafi position on washing the beard.

Core Idea
The thick beard substitutes for the skin, making its outer surface fard; the light beard requires water reach the skin.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the detailed rulings for washing the beard and other facial parts during wudu. It clarifies the distinction between thick and light beards, and specifies what must be washed and what is exempted, based on the Hanafi school's rational evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the beard categories
The passage divides beards into thick (concealing skin) and light (visible skin). Note the ruling for each.
2
Understand the legal basis
The thick beard acts as a substitute for skin; the obligation transfers to its surface. This is the rational proof.
3
Apply the distinction
For thick beard: wash the outer surface. For light beard: water must reach the skin.
4
Note the exemptions
Dangling hair beyond face circle, inner lips, inside eyes, inside healed wound are not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تمام أحكام الوضوء
يجب غسلُ ظاهرِ اللّحيةِ الكنَّةِ، في أصح ما يُفتى به. ويجب إيصال الماء إلى بَشَرةِ اللّحيةِ الخفيفة ولا يجب إيصال الماء إلى المسترسل من الشعر عن دائرة الوَجْهِ، ولا إلى ما انكتم منَ الشَّفتينِ عندَ الانضمام.
لما لم يقدم الكلام على اللحية، ذكره، فقال: (يَجِبُ) يعني: يفترض (غسلُ ظاهر اللحية الكنة)، وهي التي لا تُرى بشرتها من تحتها: (في أصَحُ مَا يُفْتَى بِهِ) من التصاحيح في حكمها، وإنما كان هذا أصح؛ لأنها قامت مقام البشرة، فتحول الفرض إليها، وما قيل غير ذلك من الاكتفاء بثلثها أو ربعها، أو مسح كلها، أو غيره، متروك؛ لأنهم رجعوا عما سوى هذا، لما قلناه. (وَيجَبُ) أي: يلزم، بمعنى: يفترض ( إِيصَالُ المَاءِ إِلى بَشَرةِ اللّحْيَةِ الخَفِيفَةِ) في المختار لبقاء المواجهة بها وعدم عسر غسلها وقيل: ويسقط لانعدام المواجهة الكاملة بالنبات. (وَلا يجب إيصال الماء إلى المسترسل مِن الشعر عن دائرة الوَجْهِ، لأنه ليس منه أصالة، وليس بدلاً عنه. (وَلا) يجب إيصال الماء (إلى مَا انْكَتَمَ مِنَ الشَّفتينِ عِنْدَ الانْضِمَامِ المعتاد، فإن المنضم تبع للفم، في الأصح، وما ظهر تبع للوجه، ولا باطن العينين ولو في الغسل، لخوف الضرر. ولا داخل قرحة برئت ولم ينفصل من قشرها سوى مخرج القيح لعدم خروجه عن حكم الباطن بهذا القدر، لأنه ضروري.
Chapter: On the complete rulings of wudu. It is obligatory (meaning: fard) to wash the apparent part of the thick beard, in the most correct view that is given fatwa upon. And it is obligatory to make water reach the skin of the light beard, and it is not obligatory to make water reach the dangling hair beyond the circle of the face, nor what is hidden between the lips when closed normally. Since he had not previously spoken about the beard, he mentioned it and said: (It is obligatory) meaning: it is fard (to wash the apparent part of the thick beard), which is that beneath which the skin is not seen: (in the most correct view that is given fatwa upon) among the corrections regarding its ruling. And this is the most correct because it (the beard) stands in place of the skin, so the obligation shifts to it. And what was said otherwise – such as being content with one-third or one-fourth, or wiping all of it, or other – is abandoned because they (the scholars) retracted from everything besides this, for what we have said. And (it is obligatory) meaning: it is required, in the sense of fard (to make water reach the skin of the light beard) in the chosen view, because the facing (of the skin) remains and there is no difficulty in washing it; and it was said: it is dropped because complete facing is non-existent due to the growth (of hair). And it is not obligatory to make water reach the dangling hair from beyond the circle of the face, because it is not originally from it (the face) and is not a substitute for it. And it is not obligatory to make water reach what is hidden from the lips when closed normally, for what is hidden follows the mouth in the most correct view, and what is apparent follows the face. And (it is not obligatory to wash) the inside of the eyes even in ghusl, due to fear of harm. And not the inside of a healed wound whose scab has not separated, except the exit of pus, because it does not exit from the ruling of the hidden part to this extent, as it is necessary.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh (commentary) explaining the rulings related to washing the beard and face in wudu. It begins by stating that it is obligatory (fard) to wash the apparent part of a thick beard—one that completely hides the skin underneath. The commentary clarifies that this is the most correct position for fatwa, because the thick beard acts as a substitute for the skin, so the obligation of washing the skin transfers to washing the outer surface of the beard. The author notes that other views (e.g., washing only one-third or one-fourth, or wiping the entire beard) have been abandoned by Hanafi scholars. Next, for a light beard where the skin is visible, it is obligatory to make water reach the skin itself, as the primary obligation is on the skin and there is no difficulty in doing so. However, if one fails to reach the skin due to thick growth, washing the apparent is sufficient. Then, the hanging hair that extends beyond the face (e.g., long beard beyond the oval) is not required to be washed, as it is not part of the face originally nor a substitute. Similarly, the hidden part of the lips when they are closed is not washed because it is considered part of the mouth's interior, not the face. The outer part of the lips is washed. The passage also mentions that the inside of the eyes is not washed even in ghusl due to potential harm, and the inside of a healed wound is not washed except for the pus exit, as it is necessary for water to reach there.

Key Terms
اللحية الكثة (al-lihyah al-kaffah): A thick beard that completely hides the skin beneath.
اللحية الخفيفة (al-lihyah al-khafīfah): A light beard where the skin is visible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a person with a light beard (where skin is visible) regarding wudu?
A) He must wash the entire beard only.
B) He must ensure water reaches the skin beneath the beard.
C) He must wipe the beard instead of washing.
D) He is exempt from washing the beard entirely.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, washing the beard in wudu depends on its thickness: thick beards require washing the outer surface, light beards require reaching the skin.

Rulings on Natural Barriers to Water in Wudu
Rulings on natural barriers in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on natural barriers such as joined fingers, long nails, and dirt under nails, and identify which barriers require removal and which are excused.

Core Idea
Natural bodily conditions like dirt under nails are excused; intentional barriers must be removed for wudu validity.

This lesson examines the first part of a passage from Nur al-Idah on barriers that prevent water from reaching the skin during wudu. The text distinguishes between natural accumulations like dirt under nails, which do not require removal, and other barriers like long nails or joined fingers that must be addressed. Understanding these distinctions ensures the fard of washing the limbs is fulfilled correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify each natural barrier mentioned
List the specific cases: joined fingers, long nails covering fingertip, dirt under nails, flea droppings, and dyer's stain.
The text also mentions dough, wax, and eye secretion as external barriers.
2
Determine the ruling for each barrier
For joined fingers and long nails, the text says 'wajib' to wash after removing the barrier. For dirt under nails, it says 'la yamna' (does not prevent). For flea droppings, also 'la yamna'. For dyer's stain, 'la yamna' due to necessity.
The key is whether the barrier prevents water from reaching the skin.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each exception
Dirt under nails is excused because it originates from the body. Flea droppings are small and porous, so water penetrates. Dyer's stain is exempt due to common necessity.
These exceptions are based on the principle of removing hardship.
4
Apply the rulings to new similar scenarios
Consider other natural accumulations like soil under a laborer's nails or sweat residue. The ruling depends on whether the substance is minimal and water can still reach the skin.
If it prevents water, it must be removed; otherwise, it is excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو انضمت الأصابع، بحيث لا يصل الماء إلى أثنائها، أو طال الظفر فغطى الأَنْمَلَةَ، فمنع وصول الماء إلى ما تحته، أو كان فيه ما يمنع الماء أن يصل إلى الجسد، كعجين وشمع ورمص يبقى خارج العين بتغميضها، وجب غسل ما تحت ذلك بعد إزالة المانع. ولا يمنع الدَّرَن، أي: الوسخ في الأظفار سواء فيه القروي والمصري، في الأصح، فيصح الغسل معه لتولده من البدن. ولا يمنع خرء البراغيث ونحوها كذنيدم الذباب، لوصول الماء إلى البدن لنفوذه فيه، لقلته وعدم لزوجته، وما على ظفر الصباغ من الصبغ، لا يمنع للضرورة، وعليه الفتوى.
ويجب تحريك الخاتم الضيق، في المختار من الروايتين؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان إذا توضأ وضوء الصلاة حرك خاتمه في إصبعه، رواه ابن ماجه، ولأنه يمنع الوصول ظاهراً. وكذا القرط في الأذن، وهو بضم القاف وإسكان الراء ما يعلق في شحمة الأذن، يتكلف لتحريكه إن كان ضيقاً، والمعتبر غلبة الظن في إيصال الماء إلى الثقب سواء كان فيه قرط أو لم يكن، فإن غلب على الظن وصول الماء إلى الثقب، لا يتكلف لغيره، من إدخال عود ونحوه في الثقب، لأن الحرج مدفوع.
ولو ضرَّه عسل شقوق رجليه جاز إمرار الماء على الدواء الذي وضعه فيها، للضرورة.
ولا يُعاد الغسل من جنابة، ولا المسح في الوضوء على موضع الشعر بعد حلقه لعدم طرو الحدث. ولا يعاد الغسل بقص ظفره وشاربه؛ لعدم الحدث بعد القص.
And if the fingers are joined such that water does not reach between them, or the nail becomes long and covers the finger tip, preventing water from reaching what is underneath, or if there is something on the body that prevents water from reaching the skin, like dough, wax, or dry secretion that remains outside the eye when it is closed, it is obligatory to wash what is underneath after removing the barrier. And dirt (i.e., filth under the nails) does not prevent, whether from a villager or a city dweller, in the most correct view, so wudu is valid with it because it originates from the body. And flea droppings and the like, such as fly excrement, do not prevent (washing) because water reaches the body due to its penetration, due to their small quantity and lack of viscosity. And what is on the nail of a dyer of dye does not prevent due to necessity, and upon this is the fatwa. And it is obligatory to move a tight ring, in the chosen of the two narrations; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he performed wudu for prayer, he moved his ring on his finger, reported by Ibn Majah, and because it apparently prevents (water) reaching. Likewise the earring in the ear – it is what is hung in the earlobe – one should exert effort to move it if it is tight. And the consideration is the predominant probability of water reaching the hole, whether there is an earring in it or not. If it is predominant that water reaches the hole, one does not exert effort for other things, like inserting a stick etc. into the hole, because hardship is repelled. And if washing cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible to pass water over the medicine he placed in them, due to necessity. And the washing (from janabah) is not repeated, nor is the wiping in wudu over the place of hair after shaving it, because no hadath occurs. Similarly, washing is not repeated by cutting his nail or mustache, because there is no hadath after cutting.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the rulings on wudu regarding barriers. It first addresses situations where water cannot reach the skin naturally. If fingers are closely joined, water does not flow between them; it is obligatory to separate them to wash the skin between. Similarly, if a nail is so long that it covers the fingertip, the covered skin must be washed after cutting or moving the nail. If there is a substance like dough, wax, or dry eye secretion that blocks water, it must be removed. However, ordinary dirt under the nails does not block water because it originates from the body and is minimal; the wudu is valid. Similarly, flea droppings and small particles like fly excrement do not prevent water because they are small and porous. A dyer's stain on nails is exempt due to necessity. Next, moving a tight ring is obligatory to ensure water reaches the finger underneath, based on a hadith and rational proof. The same applies to a tight earring; one must move it if necessary. Only if one is certain water does not reach the hole is further effort required, but general probability suffices. If someone has cracked feet and washing directly causes harm, it is permitted to apply medicine and then pass water over it. Finally, after shaving, cutting nails, or trimming the mustache, one does not need to repeat washing or wiping over those areas because no new ritual impurity (hadath) has occurred—the previous washing remains valid.

Key Terms
الدَّرَن (al-daran): Dirt or filth under the nails that originates from the body.
الخاتم الضيق (al-khatam al-dayyiq): A tight ring that prevents water from reaching the finger underneath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on dirt under the nails (al-daran)?
A) It must be removed before wudu.
B) It does not prevent the validity of wudu.
C) Only villagers are excused; city dwellers must remove it.
D) It is makruh to leave it.

Key Takeaway
Natural barriers that are minimal or originate from the body do not invalidate wudu; only barriers that physically prevent water from reaching the skin must be removed.

Rulings on Artificial Barriers and Post-Wash Issues
Rulings on artificial barriers and post-wash actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that moving a tight ring is wajib in wudu, explain the evidence, and identify cases where post-wash actions like shaving or cutting nails do not require repetition.

Core Idea
A tight ring must be moved to ensure water reaches the skin; once washed, shaving or cutting nails does not require re-washing.

This lesson continues the passage from Nur al-Idah, focusing on artificial barriers like a tight ring or earring, and the effect of post-wash actions such as shaving hair or cutting nails. The text provides a hadith for the ring ruling and clarifies that once a limb is washed, subsequent removal of hair or nails does not create a new obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on a tight ring
The text says 'يجب تحريك الخاتم الضيق' – it is wajib to move a tight ring. Note the source of obligation: a hadith from Ibn Majah and rational proof that it prevents water.
There are two narrations (riwayatayn) on this; the chosen view is obligation.
2
Apply the same principle to an earring
The text extends the ruling to earrings: if tight, one must exert effort to move it. The consideration is whether water reaches the piercing hole.
If one is confident water reaches, no further effort (like using a stick) is needed.
3
Understand the exception for necessity
If washing cracked feet causes harm, it is permissible to apply medicine and then pass water over it. This is an exception based on necessity (darurah).
This is similar to the earlier exception for dyer's stain.
4
Analyze the ruling on post-wash actions
After washing, shaving hair or cutting nails does not require repeating the wash or wipe because no new hadath (ritual impurity) occurs. The previous washing remains valid.
This applies to both ghusl and wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rulings on Obstacles in Wudu: Tight Ring, Earring, and Medication
Expansion of rulings on obstacles in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on moving a tight ring during wudu, explain when passing water over medication is permissible, and identify why no rewash is required after shaving or cutting nails.

Core Idea
Water must reach the skin; obstacles that prevent water require moving (wajib), but necessity permits passing water over medication.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah expands on Nur al-Idah, addressing practical scenarios where obstacles might prevent water from reaching the skin during wudu. You will learn the obligation to move a tight ring or earring, the concession for applying water over medication on cracks, and the rule that shaving or cutting nails does not require repeating wudu. These rulings balance the requirement of washing all parts with the principle of removing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
List the four scenarios: tight ring/earring, medication on cracks, shaving hair, cutting nails/dye. Note the Hanafi ruling for each: wajib to move, ja'iz to pass water, no rewash needed, no obstruction from dye.
Each scenario has its own condition and hukm.
2
Locate the dalil for the ring
The hadith from Ibn Majah: the Prophet used to move his ring during wudu. This is the primary evidence. Understand that the Hanafi school uses this as proof of obligation (wujub) because it is ahad.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut, so it yields wajib, not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action is interpreted as teaching that water must reach under the ring. Since moving the ring is necessary to fulfill the wudu requirement, it becomes wajib. The general command to wash the hands includes the skin under the ring.
If moving causes harm, the concession applies.
4
Apply the principle of necessity for medication
If water directly on cracks causes harm, one may pass water over the medication. This is based on raf' al-haraj (removal of hardship). The same logic applies to any water-resistant ointment.
The key condition is genuine harm; mere dislike is insufficient.
5
Understand the post-shaving and nail-cutting ruling
Shaving hair or cutting nails does not create a new hadath; the original washing already covered those areas. Therefore, no rewash or wipe is needed. Dye on nails is not an obstacle if water penetrates due to thinness.
This prevents unnecessary repetition of acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب تحريك الخاتم الضيق. ولو ضرَّهُ عَسَلُ شُقُوقِ رِجْلَيْهِ، جازَ إمرارُ الماءِ على الدواء الذي وضعَهُ فيها. ولا يُعادُ الغَسْلُ ولا المسحُ على موضعِ الشعرِ بعد حَلْقِهِ، وَلا الغَسْلُ بقصُ ظُفْرِه وشارِبِهِ.
وصول الماء إلى البدن لنفوذه فيه، لقلته وعدم لزوجته، وما على ظفر الصباغ من الصبغ، لا يمنع للضرورة، وعليه الفتوى. (وَيَجِبُ) أي: يلزم ( تَحْرِيكُ الخَاتَم الضَّيقِ)، في المخـتـار مـن الـروايـتـيـن؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ إِذَا تَوَضَّأَ وُضُوءَ الصَّلاةِ حَرَّكَ خَاتَمَهُ فِي إِصْبَعِهِ » ، رواه ابن ماجه، ولأنه يمنع الوصول ظاهراً. وكذا القرط في الأذن، وهو بضم القاف وإسكان الـراء مـا يـعـلـق في شحمة الأذن، يتكلف لتحريكه إن كان ضيقاً، والمعتبر غلبة الظن في إيصال الماء إلى الثقب سواء كان فيه قرط أو لم يكن، فإن غلب على الظن وصول الماء إلى الثقب، لا يتكلف لغيره، من إدخــال عــود ونحوه في الثقب، لأن الحرج مدفوع، (ولو ضرَّهُ عَسَلُ شُقُوقِ رِجْلَيْهِ جازَ) أي: صح (إمْرَارُ المَاءِ عَلَى الدّواء الَّذِي وَضَعَهُ فِيهَا) أي: الشقوق، للضرورة، ( ولا يُعادُ الغَسْلُ من جنابة، (ولا المَسحُ) في الوضوء (على موضعِ الشّعرِ بعد حَلْقِهِ) لعدم طرو الحدث. (وَ) كذا ( لا ) يعاد (الغَسْلُ بقص ظُفْره وشاريه؛ لعدم الحدث بعد القص.
It is obligatory to move the tight ring. And if the honey of the cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible to pass water over the medicine that he placed in them. And the washing and wiping over the place of hair after shaving it is not repeated, nor the washing by cutting his nail and mustache. The arrival of water to the body due to its penetration into it, because of its scarcity and lack of viscosity, and what is on the nail of the dyer of dye, does not prevent due to necessity, and upon it is the fatwa. (And it is obligatory) i.e., required (to move the tight ring), in the chosen of the two narrations; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to, when he performed wudu for prayer, move his ring on his finger, narrated by Ibn Majah, and because it prevents arrival apparently. Likewise the earring in the ear, and it is with damma on qaf and sukun on ra' what hangs from the earlobe, one undertakes to move it if it is tight, and the consideration is the preponderance of assumption in delivering water to the hole whether there is an earring in it or not, for if he assumes water reaches the hole, he does not undertake anything else, like inserting a stick or similar into the hole, because hardship is repelled. (And if the honey of the cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible) i.e., valid (to pass water over the medicine that he placed in them) i.e., the cracks, due to necessity. (And the washing is not repeated from janabah, nor the wiping) in wudu (on the place of hair after shaving it) because no hadath occurs. (And) likewise (the washing is not repeated) by cutting his nail and mustache; because no hadath occurs after cutting.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is an expansion of rulings from Nur al-Idah. It addresses several practical issues in wudu regarding obstacles to water reaching the skin. First, it states the obligation to move a tight ring during wudu so water reaches under it. This is the chosen position from two narrations, supported by a hadith from Ibn Majah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would move his ring. Similarly, an earring that is tight must be moved unless one is reasonably certain water reaches the hole. The second ruling: if one has cracked feet and applies medication, and water would harm the cracks, it is permissible to simply pass water over the medication without removing it, due to necessity. Third, after shaving hair or cutting nails, one does not need to rewash or rewipe those areas because the cutting does not create a new hadath; the previous washing covers the area. Finally, a note on water reaching the body through porous substances: if a dyer has dye on his nails, it does not prevent validity of wudu due to necessity, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. The overall approach balances the requirement to ensure water reaches the skin with the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj).

Key Terms
الخاتم الضيق (al-khatam al-dayyiq): A ring that is tight on the finger, preventing water from reaching the skin underneath during wudu.
القرط (al-qurt): An earring worn in the earlobe; if tight, must be moved during wudu.
عَسَلُ شُقُوقِ رِجْلَيْهِ (asal shuquq rijlayhi): A medicinal substance (possibly honey) applied to cracked feet; if water harms, one may pass water over it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a tight ring during wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is sunnah to move it.
B) It is wajib to move it.
C) It is makruh to move it.
D) It is haram to wear a ring during wudu.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, obstacles to water during wudu must be moved if they prevent access, but necessity allows concessions; shaving or cutting nails does not require recleansing.

Sunnah Acts of Wudu: Definition and First Two Sunnahs
Definition of sunnah and first two sunan of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sunnah technically in Hanafi fiqh and explain the ruling and scope of washing the hands to the wrists and saying Bismillah in wudu.

Core Idea
Sunnah in wudu is a recommended practice; its omission does not invalidate but loses reward.

This passage opens the chapter on the sunan (recommended acts) of wudu. The author first defines the term 'sunnah' both linguistically and technically, distinguishing three types based on the Prophet's consistency and associated threat. Then he begins listing the eighteen sunan, starting with washing the hands to the wrists and saying 'Bismillah' at the beginning. Understanding this foundation helps you distinguish what is essential (fard/wajib) from what is meritorious (sunnah) in wudu.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the definition of sunnah
Break down the linguistic meaning (customary way) and technical meaning (consistent religious practice without obligation). Identify the three subcategories: mu'akkadah (emphasized, with occasional omission), mandubah (done sometimes), and what is accompanied by a threat (indicating obligation).
The Hanafi technical definition emphasizes 'without obligation' and 'on the basis of continuity'.
2
Examine the first sunnah: washing hands to wrists
Identify that this is sunnah for all, not only for those waking from sleep. Note that it is more emphasized after sleep due to the hadith. Understand the joint term 'rusgh' and that the washing includes the wrist area.
3
Evaluate the hadith evidence
Analyze the hadith from Abu Hurairah in Sahihayn. See how it supports the emphasis for one who wakes: the prohibition of dipping until washed three times. Understand the rationale (not knowing where the hand spent the night).
4
Understand the method of washing hands
Learn the prescribed method: lift vessel with left hand, pour on right three times, then switch. If vessel is large, tilt it. If no confirmed impurity, insert fingers (not full palm) to avoid making water musta'mal.
5
Apply the ruling for tasmiyah (Bismillah)
Determine that the sunnah is to say 'Bismillah' at the very beginning. If forgotten and remembered during wudu, saying it later does not achieve the sunnah, though it remains recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في سنن الوضوء (يُسَنُ في حال الوضوء ثمانيةَ عَشَرَ شيئاً ذكر العدد تسهيلاً للمتعلم، لأنه ليس للحصر حقيقة، والسنة لغة: الطريقة المعتادة، ولو سيئة، واصطلاحاً: الطريقة المسلوكة في الديـن مـن غـيـر لزوم، على سبيل المواظبة. ثم إن كانت مما واظب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع الترك، فهي المؤكدة، وإن كان معه أحياناً فهي المندوبة، وإن اقترنت بوعيد لمن لم يفعلها فهي للوجوب، فيسن (غسل اليدين إلى الرُّسْغَينِ)، ابتداء الرسغ - بضم الراء وسكون السين المهملة والغين المعجمة-: المفصـل الـذي الساعد والكف، والذي بين الساق والقدم، وسواء استيقظ من نوم، أو لم يكن نائماً، لأن من حكى وضوء النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، قدمه، وإنما يحكي ما كان دأبه وعادته عليه الصلاة والسلام، لا خصوص وضوئه الذي هو عن نومه ولكن يكون الغسل أكد في حق من استيقظ، وكان غير مستنج بالماء، أو كان على بدنه نجاسة، لما في الصحيحين عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: « إِذَا اسْتَيْقَظَ أحَدُكُمْ مِنْ مَنَامِهِ، فَلا يَغْمِس يَدَهُ فِي الإِنَاءِ حَتَّى يَغْسِلَهَا ». ولفظ مسلم: « حَتَّى يَغْسِلَهَا ثَلاثًا، فَإِنَّهُ لا يَدْرِي أَيْنَ بَاتَتْ يَدُهُ ». وورد مؤكداً بالنون « فَلا يَعْمِسَنَّ يَدَهُ فِي طَهُورِهِ حَتَّى يُفْرِغْ عَلَيْهَا ثَلاثاً » وكيفيته: أن يرفع الإناء بيسراه، ويصب الماء على يمناه ثلاثاً، ثم يفعل كذلك باليمنى على اليسار، وإن كان الإناء كبيراً يميله، وإن لم يمكن إمالته، وليس على يديه نجاسة متحققة، يدخل أصابع اليسرى مضمومة، دون الكف لوقوع الكفاية بالأصابع، ويصب على اليمنى، ثم يدلك أصابعها ببعضها، ثم يدخل اليمنى ويغسل يسراه، وإن زاد على قدر الضرورة بإدخال الكف، صار الماء مستعملاً. (والتسمية ابتداء) حتى لو نسي، فتذكرها في خلال الوضوء فسمى لا تحصل السنة)،
Chapter on the sunan of wudu. (Eighteen things are sunnah in the state of wudu; mentioning the number is to facilitate the student, for it is not actually limited. Sunnah linguistically is the customary way, even if evil, and technically: the way followed in religion without obligation, on the basis of continuity. Then if it is among what the Prophet (peace be upon him) consistently did, with occasional omission, it is mu'akkadah, and if he did it sometimes, it is mandubah, and if it is accompanied by a threat for the one who does not do it, then it is for obligation. So it is sunnah (to wash the hands to the wrists). Al-rusgh - with damma on ra', sukun on sin, and ghayn mu'jamah - is the joint that connects the forearm and palm, and that which connects the shin and foot. It applies whether one wakes from sleep or is not sleeping, because whoever narrated the wudu of the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned it, and he only narrates what was his consistent habit and custom, not specifically his wudu after sleep. But the washing is more emphasized for one who wakes, especially if he did not perform istinja' with water or if there is impurity on his body, due to what is in the Sahihayn from Abu Hurairah: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you wakes from his sleep, let him not dip his hand into the vessel until he washes it.' And Muslim's wording: 'Until he washes it three times, for he does not know where his hand spent the night.' And it is reported with emphasis by the nun: 'Let him not dip his hand into his purifier until he pours upon it three times.' Its method: he lifts the vessel with his left hand and pours water over his right hand three times, then does likewise with the right on the left. If the vessel is large, he tilts it. If he cannot tilt it and there is no confirmed impurity on his hands, he inserts the fingers of his left hand, pressed together, without the palm, because the fingers suffice, and pours on the right, then rubs its fingers together, then inserts the right and washes the left. If he exceeds the necessary amount by inserting the palm, the water becomes used. (And the tasmiyah at the beginning) so that if he forgets and remembers during wudu and says it, the sunnah is not achieved.)
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the sunan (recommended acts) of wudu in the Sharh. It first defines the term 'sunnah' linguistically and technically: technically, it is a religious practice that is not obligatory but performed consistently. Three types are distinguished: sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized, regularly done by the Prophet with occasional omission), sunnah mandubah (recommended, done sometimes), and if a threat accompanies omission, it indicates obligation (wajib). Then it begins enumerating the 18 sunan. The first is washing hands to the wrists (rusghayn), which is sunnah for everyone, not only for one waking from sleep. However, it is more emphasized for the one who wakes, especially if he did not use water for istinja' or has impurity on his body, supported by the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim about not dipping the hand until it is washed three times. The method of washing is described: pour with left hand on right three times, then left hand from right. The ruling that if one inserts the whole palm unnecessarily, the water becomes musta'mal (used) is noted. The second sunnah is saying 'Bismillah' at the beginning; if forgotten, saying it during wudu does not fulfill the sunnah (though it is still recommended as a mandub).

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): Technically, a religious practice followed consistently without obligation, on the basis of continuity. Includes mu'akkadah, mandubah, and if with threat, wajib.
الرسغ (al-rusgh): The joint connecting the forearm and palm (wrist) or the shin and foot (ankle).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone forgets to say 'Bismillah' at the beginning of wudu and remembers during wudu, what is the ruling?
A) The sunnah is fulfilled by saying it later.
B) The sunnah is not achieved for the tasmiyah.
C) The wudu is invalid.
D) He must restart wudu.

Key Takeaway
Sunnah acts in wudu enhance the act but are not obligatory; their omission does not invalidate wudu.

Basmalah in Wudu: Sunnah and Timing
Explains the ruling and timing of saying Bismillah in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of tasmiyah in wudu, explain the Hanafi interpretation of the supporting hadith, and identify the correct timing for saying it.

Core Idea
Saying Bismillah at the start of wudu is sunnah, not wajib; the 'no wudu' hadith negates virtue, not validity.

The obligation of basmalah in wudu is a point of debate among schools. The Hanafi position, grounded in a careful reading of the primary texts, holds it as a confirmed sunnah. This lesson unpacks the evidence and explains the recommended practice of saying it twice for precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (tasmiyah at the start of wudu) and the Hanafi hukm level (sunnah muakkadah).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'No wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name.' Note its chain and strength (ahad, zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the Hanafis interpret the phrase 'no wudu' as negation of virtue (fadilah) rather than validity (jawaz), supported by the hadith on partial purification.
Key: the second hadith clarifies the first.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view (some scholars consider it wajib) and the divergent interpretations of the same hadith.
The thamarah: validity of wudu without tasmiyah.
5
Evaluate the preferred Hanafi practice
Understand the recommendation to say tasmiyah twice (before istinja and after covering) as a precaution against the differing opinions on timing.
Qadi Khan affirms this as al-asahh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف نحوه في الأكل؛ لأن الوضوء عمل واحد، بخلاف الأكل ونحوه، كذا في « الغاية»، وقــال المحقق « الكمال بن الهمام : وهو إنما يستلزم في الأكل تحصيل السنة في الباقي، لاستدراك ما فات. انتهى. وقال شارح المنية بعد نقله : والأولى أنه استدراك لما فات بالحديث، وهو قولـه عليـه الصلاة والسلام : ( إذا أكل أحدكمْ فَنَسِيَ أَنْ يَذْكُرَ اسم الله عَلَى طَعَامِهِ، فَلَيَقُلْ: بِسمِ اللهِ أَوَّلِهِ وآخره). رواه أبو داود والترمذي. ولا حديث في الوضوء. انتهى. وإنما كانت التسمية سنة في أوله لقوله : ( لا صَلاةَ لِمَنْ لا وُضُوءَ لَهُ، ولا وُضُوءَ لِمَنْ لَمْ يَذْكُر اسم الله عَليهِ) . والـمــاد نـفـي الفضيلة، لا نفي الجواز ، لقوله : ( مَنْ تَوَضًا وَذكَرَ اسْمَ اللهِ فَإِنَّهُ يُطَهُرُ جَسَدَهُ كله، وَمَنْ تَوَضًا وَلَمْ يَذْكُرْ اسمَ اللَّهِ لَمْ يُطَهِّرْ إِلا مُوضِعَ الوُضُوءِ). والمنقول عن السلف، وقيل: عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في لفظها : بسم الله العظيم، والحمد لله على دين الإسلام ، وقيل : الأفضل ( بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم لعموم قوله : ( كُلَّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَال لا يُبْدَأ فِيهِ ببسم الله الرحمنِ الرَّحِيمِ، فَهُوَ أَبْتَرُ ). والأصح، أنه يسمي مرتين، مرة قبل كشف العورة للاستنجاء، ومرة بعد سترها، عند ابتداء غسل سائر الأعضاء، احتياطاً للخلاف الواقع فيها. فإن بعضهم قال : يسمي قبل الاستنجاء فقط. وقال بعضهم: يسمي ! فحسب، لأن قبل الاستنجاء حال كشف العورة، وذكره تعالى حال كشفها غير مستحب. قال قاضي خان : والأصح أنه يسمي مرتين.
Contrary to its like in eating because wudu is a single act, unlike eating and its like, as in al-Ghayah. And the investigator Kamal ibn al-Humam said: It only necessitates in eating the attainment of the sunnah in the remaining part, to make up for what was missed. End. And the commentator of al-Munyah said after quoting: The better view is that it is making up for what was missed by the hadith, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'When one of you eats and forgets to mention Allah's name over his food, let him say: In the name of Allah, its beginning and its end.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And there is no hadith concerning wudu. End. And the tasmiyah is only sunnah at its beginning due to his saying: 'There is no prayer for one who has no wudu, and no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name over it.' And the meaning is negation of virtue, not negation of permissibility, due to his saying: 'Whoever performs wudu and mentions Allah's name, then he purifies his entire body, and whoever performs wudu and does not mention Allah's name, he only purifies the place of wudu.' And what is transmitted from the Salaf, and it is said: from the Prophet, peace be upon him, in its wording: 'In the name of Allah the Great, and praise be to Allah for the religion of Islam.' And it is said: the best is 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' due to the generality of his saying: 'Every matter of importance that is not begun with In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful is cut off.' And the most correct is that he says it twice: once before uncovering the private parts for istinja, and once after covering them, at the beginning of washing the rest of the limbs, as a precaution regarding the difference occurring therein. For some said: he says it before istinja only. And some said: he says it only, because before istinja is the state of uncovering the private parts, and mentioning Him, exalted, during uncovering is not recommended. Qadi Khan said: The most correct is that he says it twice.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the sunnah of saying Bismillah at the start of wudu. The commentator begins by distinguishing wudu from eating: in eating, if one forgets to say Bismillah at the beginning, he can say it later to attain the sunnah, based on a hadith from Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. However, there is no such hadith for wudu, meaning that if one forgets, the sunnah is lost. The tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) is sunnah at the beginning of wudu, as the hadith 'No wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name' indicates. The commentator clarifies that this hadith negates virtue (fadilah), not permissibility (jawaz), as another hadith shows that without tasmiyah, only the limbs washed are purified, not the whole body. There is a difference of opinion on the wording: some transmitted from the Salaf 'Bismillah al-‘Azim wa al-Hamdulillah ‘ala din al-Islam', but others prefer 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' due to a general hadith. The most correct position (al-asahh) adopted by Qadi Khan is to say it twice: once before uncovering the private parts for istinja, and once after covering them when washing the other limbs. This is precautionary (ihtiyatan) due to the disagreement among scholars—some requiring it only before istinja, others considering it disliked to mention Allah while uncovering the private parts. The Hanafi position is that tasmiyah is sunnah, and the most cautious practice is to say it twice.

Key Terms
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): The act of saying 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) at the beginning of wudu.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ritual washing of specific body parts before prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the most correct (al-asahh) practice regarding saying Bismillah in wudu?
A) Say it once at the beginning of wudu.
B) Say it twice: once before istinja and once after covering the private parts.
C) Say it only after washing the face.
D) It is not required at all.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu is a confirmed sunnah; omitting it does not invalidate wudu, but the sunnah reward is lost.

Siwak: Definition, Ruling, and Proper Method in the Hanafi School
Defines siwak and explains its ruling and proper use with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on siwak (sunnah mu'akkadah), identify its proper method, and apply it in various recommended situations.

Core Idea
Siwak is a confirmed sunnah of wudu and the religion, but not obligatory, and its virtue extends beyond prayer to many states.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah defines siwak, establishes its sunnah status through prophetic hadith, and details the recommended physical characteristics and method of use. It also clarifies that siwak is a sunnah of wudu in the Hanafi school (not merely of prayer) and is recommended in numerous situations beyond wudu, reflecting Abu Hanifah's view that it is a sunnah of the religion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of siwak
Distinguish between the act (istiyak) and the stick (miswak). The passage uses siwak to mean the act of cleaning teeth with the stick.
2
Determine the ruling and its proof
The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah. The proof is the hadith 'Were it not that I would burden my community...' which shows the Prophet refrained from commanding it due to hardship, implying it is desirable but not obligatory.
Also the hadith that prayer with siwak is seventy times more virtuous.
3
Examine the recommended physical characteristics
The siwak should be soft, finger-thickness, a handspan long, straight, few knots, from the arak tree. These features optimize its cleaning benefits.
The specified tree is arak (Salvadora persica).
4
Understand the method of use
Widthwise motion is preferred (majority view), at the time of madmadah (rinsing mouth) during wudu. The grip: little finger below, ring and index above, thumb below tip, held in right hand. Avoid tight grip.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Ghaznawi permits both lengthwise and widthwise.
5
Note the scope and substitutes
Siwak is a sunnah of wudu (not prayer) in the Hanafi school, so its virtue applies to all prayers performed with that wudu without repeating. It is also recommended in other states: change of mouth, after sleep, before prayer, entering home, gatherings, reciting Quran. Substitutes: finger or rough cloth when siwak is unavailable; gum for women.
This shows siwak is a sunnah of the religion per Abu Hanifah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والسواك) - بكسر السين- اسم الاستياك، وللعود الذي يستاك به أيضاً. والمراد هنا الأول. يقال: ساكَ فاه يسوكه إذا دلكَهُ بالمسواك، وإذا لم يذكــر الفــم، قلت: استاك، وإنما كان سنة لقوله : ( لَوْلا أَنْ أَشْقَ عَلَى أُمَّتِي، لأمَرْتُهُمْ بِالسَّوَاكِ عِنْدَ كُلِّ صَلَاةٍ). أو « مَعَ كُلِّ صَلَاةٍ » ). ولما ورد « أنَّ كُلَّ صَلَاةٍ بِهِ تفضلُ سَبْعِينَ صَلاة بدونـه » . وينبغي أن يـكـون ليناً في غلط الإصبع، طول شبر مستوياً، قليل العقد من الأشجار المعروفة، وهي: الأراك، ليكون أقطع للبلغم، وأنقى للصدر، وأهني للطعام، وأن يستاك عرضاً، لا طولاً، لئلا يضـر لـحـم الأسـنان، وعليه الأكثر. وقال « الغزنوي » : يستاك طولاً وعرضاً ( في ابتدائـه)، أي الوضوء. ونص عليه، لأن الابتداء به سنة أيضاً. ووقته عند المضمضة على قول الأكثر. وقال غيرهم قبل الوضوء، والسواك من سنن الوضوء عندنا لا من سنن الصلاة، فيحصل فضيلته في كل صلاة صلاها بوضوء استاك فيـه مـن غير استياك عند قيامه لها. وليس السواك من خصائص الوضوء، فإنه يستحب في حالات، منها تغير الفم، والقيام من النوم، وإلى الصلاة، ودخول البيت واجتماع الناس، وقراءة القرآن والحديث، لقول أبي حنيفة: إن السواك من سنن الدين، فيستوي فيه الأحوال كلها. وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: السَّوَاكُ مَطْهَرَةٌ لِلْفَمِ، مَرْضَاةٌ لِلرَّبِّ». وفضيلة السواك تحصل (وَلَو) كان الاستياك (بالإصبع)، أو خرقة خشنة (عند فقده)، أي: السواك أو فقد أسنانه، أو ضرر بفمه. لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: يُجْزِئُ مِنَ السَّوَاكِ الأصَابِعُ ) . وقال علي : « التشويص) بالمسبحة والإبـهام ســواك». ويقــوم العلك مقامه للنساء، لرقة بشرتهن ويستحب إمساكه باليد اليمني والسنة في كيفية أخذه، أن تجعل الخنصر من يمينك أسفل السواك، والبنصر والسبابة فوقه، واجعل الإبهام أسفل رأسه. كما رواه ابن مسعود ). ولا تقبض القبضة على السواك، فإنه يورث الباسور).
And as for siwak – with a kasrah on the seen – it is the noun for the act of using the siwak, and also for the stick itself. What is meant here is the former. It is said: he saaka his mouth, i.e., he rubbed it with the miswak, and if he does not mention the mouth, you say: he istaka. And it is only a sunnah due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Were it not that I would burden my community, I would have commanded them to use the siwak with every prayer.' Or 'with every prayer.' And because it has been reported that 'every prayer with it is superior to seventy prayers without it.' And it should be soft in the thickness of a finger, a handspan in length, straight, with few knots, from known trees, which is the arak tree, so that it may cut phlegm, cleanse the chest, and make food more palatable. And one should use it widthwise, not lengthwise, lest it harm the gums, and this is the view of the majority. And al-Ghaznawi said: He uses it lengthwise and widthwise at the beginning, i.e., of wudu. And he stated it explicitly, because beginning with it is also a sunnah. Its time is at the rinsing of the mouth according to the majority. And others said: before wudu. And siwak is from the sunnan of wudu in our school, not from the sunnan of prayer, so its virtue is attained in every prayer performed with a wudu in which he used siwak, even if he does not use it again when standing for it. And siwak is not exclusive to wudu; rather, it is recommended in various situations, such as change of mouth, rising from sleep, going to prayer, entering the house, gatherings of people, and recitation of Quran and hadith, due to the statement of Abu Hanifah: 'Siwak is from the sunan of the religion, so it is equal in all states.' And he, peace be upon him, said: 'Siwak is a purification for the mouth and a pleasure for the Lord.' And the virtue of siwak is attained even if one uses a finger or a rough cloth when the siwak is unavailable, i.e., the siwak or the loss of one's teeth or harm to one's mouth. Due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The fingers suffice from the siwak.' And Ali said: 'Rubbing with the index finger and thumb is siwak.' And gum (cud) takes its place for women, due to the softness of their skin. And it is recommended to hold it with the right hand, and the sunnah in how to take it is to place the little finger of your right hand below the siwak, the ring finger and index finger above it, and place the thumb below its tip, as narrated by Ibn Mas'ud. And do not grip the siwak tightly, for it causes hemorrhoids.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof defining siwak and explaining its sunnah status and method. Siwak is a sunnah of wudu in the Hanafi school, not of prayer itself, meaning its reward is tied to the wudu one performs. However, it is not exclusive to wudu; it is recommended in many states: when the mouth changes, after sleep, before prayer, when entering the home, in gatherings, and before reciting Quran or hadith. The proof is the hadith that the Prophet would have commanded it if not for hardship, and the report that prayer with siwak is seventy times more virtuous. The preferred siwak is from the arak tree, of a finger's thickness, a handspan long, straight, soft, and used widthwise (to protect gums). Some say to use it both ways at the start of wudu. Its time is at rinsing the mouth (madmadah) according to most. If siwak is unavailable, one may use a finger or rough cloth; for women, gum (al-'ilk) substitutes. The proper grip: little finger below, ring and index above, thumb below the tip, held in the right hand, avoiding a tight grip which causes hemorrhoids. The virtue of siwak is confirmed as a general sunnah of the religion per Abu Hanifah, applicable in all situations.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): The act of cleaning the teeth with a stick (miswak) or the stick itself; in this passage, the act is meant.
الاستياك (al-istiyak): The verbal noun meaning the act of using the siwak.
الأراك (al-arak): A specific tree (Salvadora persica) whose twigs are traditionally used as miswak.
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth, which is the preferred time for using siwak during wudu according to the majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of siwak in the Hanafi school?
A) Wajib
B) Sunnah mu'akkadah
C) Fard
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Siwak is a confirmed sunnah of wudu and the religion, recommended in many states, with specific method and benefits; its virtue is tied to wudu, not each prayer individually.

Taharah — Purification
