Sunnahs of Dalk, Wala', and Niyyah in Wudu
Defines three sunnahs with proofs and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for dalk, wala', and niyyah in wudu, identify their proofs from the Prophet's practice, and distinguish between sunnah and fard based on the nature of the evidence.

Core Idea
Rubbing, continuity, and intention are sunnah in wudu—omission is valid but misses reward.

The performance of wudu involves more than just washing the required limbs. The Hanafi school highlights three sunnah acts—rubbing (dalk), sequential continuity (wala'), and intention (niyyah)—that enhance the act. Understanding their ruling and proof helps you avoid common misconceptions and prioritize what is obligatory versus recommended.

Guided Path
1
Locate the ruling for each sunnah
From the passage, identify the hukm (ruling) for dalk, wala', and niyyah. The phrase 'ويسن' (it is sunnah) is repeated for each.
All three are explicitly stated as sunnah.
2
Examine the proof (dalil) for each
The passage cites the Prophet's practice (فعل النبي) and his persistence (مواظبته) as evidence. Determine why this indicates sunnah and not obligation.
Persistent practice without explicit command yields sunnah in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's continuous action shows it is recommended, but the Quranic command is general (غسل) without conditioning it on rubbing, continuity, or intention. This absence of condition means it is not fard.
Contrast with Shafi'i view that niyyah is fard based on other texts.
4
Understand the definition of wala'
Wala' means washing the next limb before the previous one dries under normal conditions. The passage includes an exception for excuses (e.g., water running out). Identify what constitutes a valid excuse and how it affects the sunnah.
The sunnah is not broken when there is a legitimate reason.
5
Dissect the definition of niyyah
The passage provides linguistic and technical definitions of niyyah, emphasizing that it is a type of will (إرادة), not mere knowledge. Understand why it includes restraining from prohibitions and how it differs from other will-related terms.
This clarifies that intention is an act of the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) يسن (الدَّلْكُ) وهو : أن يمر يده على العضو بعد غسله. وكان سنة لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام فعله (۳)، ومواظبته عليه دليل السنة، دون الفرض، لأن الله تعالى أمر بالغسل مطلقاً عن شرط الدلك. (وَ) يسن (الولاء) - بكسر الواو - المتابعة، وهو: أن يغسل العضو الثاني قبل جفاف الأول في زمان معتدل وبدن معتدل )، وقيل: أن لا يشتغل بينهما بعمل آخر بغير عذر، بأن فرغ الماء، أو انقلب الإناء، فذهب لطلب غيره، وما شبهه. فإن كان لا بأس به لمواظبة النبي الله عليه . (و) يسن ( النّيّةُ). والكلام عليها من وجوه، حقيقتها، ووقتها، وصفتها، وكيفيتها، ومحلها. أما حقيقتها لغة فعزم القلب على الشيء. واصطلاحاً: توجـه القـلـب نحو إيجاد الفعل جزماً. ودخل فيه كف النفس عن المنهي عنه، لأن كفها إيجاد فعل، لأنه لا تكليف إلا بفعل على الراجح، وبه يندفع قول بعضهم: إن المكلف به في النهي ليس هو الكف الذي هو الانتهاء. وبما قلناه علم أن النية معنى وراء العلم، فهي نوع إرادة، كالقصد والعزيمة، والهـم والـحـب والــود، فالكل اسم للإرادة الحادثة، لكن العزم: اسم للمتقدم على الفعل، والقصد : اسم للمقترن بالفعل
And it is sunnah to rub (dalk), which is to pass the hand over the limb after washing it. It was sunnah because the Prophet (PBUH) did it and persisted upon it, which indicates sunnah, not obligation, because Allah commanded washing absolutely without the condition of rubbing. And it is sunnah to maintain continuity (wala') - with a kasra on the waw - meaning to follow sequentially, which is that one washes the second limb before the first dries in moderate time and moderate body condition. And it is said: that one does not engage in another action between them without an excuse, such as if the water runs out or the vessel spills, then he goes to seek other water and the like - then there is no harm because of the Prophet's persistence. And it is sunnah to have intention (niyyah). Discussion about it has aspects: its reality, time, description, modality, and place. As for its reality linguistically: it is the resolution of the heart upon something. Technically: the direction of the heart towards performing the act with determination. It includes restraining the self from what is prohibited, because restraining is an act, since there is no obligation except with an act in the sound view, and by this the statement of some is refuted that what is required in prohibitions is not the restraint which is desistance. And by what we have said, it is known that the intention is a meaning beyond knowledge; it is a type of will, like purpose, resolution, aspiration, love, and affection - all are names for the created will. But 'resolution' is the name for that which precedes the action, and 'purpose' is the name for that which coincides with the action.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines three sunnah acts of wudu: dalk (rubbing limbs after washing), wala' (continuity between washes), and niyyah (intention). For each, the author provides the ruling (sunnah) and proof from the Prophet's practice. Dalk is sunnah because the Prophet did it, but it is not fard since the Quran only mentions washing. Wala' is defined as washing the next limb before the previous dries under normal conditions; however, if there is an excuse (like running out of water), the connection is still valid. Niyyah is discussed in detail: its linguistic and technical definitions, and a clarification that intention is a type of will, not mere knowledge. The author refutes the claim that in prohibitions the required action is not the restraint, arguing that restraint is indeed an act. The key Hanafi principle highlighted is that niyyah in wudu is sunnah, not fard (unlike the Shafi'i school).

Key Terms
الدلك (al-dalk): Passing the hand over the washed limb to rub it; sunnah in wudu.
الولاء (al-walāʾ): Continuity in wudu, washing successive limbs before the previous dries.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention, the resolve of the heart to perform the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of intending (niyyah) for wudu?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mustahab

Key Takeaway
Dalk, wala', and niyyah are sunnah acts in wudu—they are recommended but not obligatory; omitting them does not invalidate wudu.

Rubbing the Limbs After Washing in Wudu
Definition and ruling of dalk as sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of dalk (rubbing) in wudu and explain why it is considered sunnah rather than fard in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Rubbing is a sunnah act; the absolute command to wash excludes it from being obligatory.

In this lesson, we examine the sunnah act of dalk (rubbing) during wudu. The author of Nur al-Idah defines it and clarifies its legal status. Understanding why it is sunnah and not fard hinges on the nature of the Quranic command and the evidence from the Prophet's practice. This distinction is essential for proper wudu performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, identify the specific act (dalk) and the ruling assigned to it by the Hanafi school. The text explicitly states it is sunnah.
Dalk is defined as passing the hand over the limb after washing.
2
Locate the primary proof
The author cites two pieces of evidence: the Prophet's actual performance (fa'ala) and his persistence upon it (muwazabah). These indicate sunnah, not obligation.
The hadith quoted is from al-Darqutni.
3
Analyze the reasoning for sunnah status
The author argues that obligation (fard) requires a clear command. Since the Quran says 'wash your faces and hands' (5:6) absolutely, without mentioning rubbing, rubbing cannot be obligatory. The Prophet's consistent practice elevates it to sunnah.
The absolute command is key: 'فاغسلوا' without condition.
4
Distinguish between fard and sunnah in wudu
Understand that things like washing the face are fard; omitting fard invalidates wudu. Sunnah acts like dalk are recommended; omitting them does not invalidate but one loses reward.
This distinction applies across Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to practice
When performing wudu, one should make sure to perform dalk after washing each limb to follow the sunnah. If forgotten, wudu is still valid.
It is a recommended practice, not a requirement for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) يسن (الدَّلْكُ) وهو : أن يمر يده على العضو بعد غسله. وكان سنة لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام فعله (۳)، ومواظبته عليه دليل السنة، دون الفرض، لأن الله تعالى أمر بالغسل مطلقاً عن شرط الدلك.
(And) it is sunnah (rubbing), and it is: that he passes his hand over the limb after washing it. And it was sunnah because the Prophet, peace be upon him, did it, and his persistence upon it is evidence of sunnah, not obligation, because Allah the Exalted commanded washing absolutely without the condition of rubbing.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the sunnah act of dalk (rubbing) in wudu. It is the action of passing the hand over the washed limb after pouring water. The author argues it is sunnah, not fard, because the Quranic command to wash is unconditional; rubbing is not mentioned. The proof is the Prophet's practice and his persistence, which indicates it is a recommended act, not an obligation.

Key Terms
الدَّلْك (al-dalk): Passing the hand over the limb after washing during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Rubbing (dalk) in wudu is a sunnah act, not obligatory, because the Quranic command to wash is unconditional and the Prophet's persistent practice only elevates it to sunnah.

Continuity (Muwalat) in Wudu: Definition and Sunnah Ruling
Definition of muwalat and its ruling as a sunnah act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of muwalat and explain why it is considered sunnah rather than fard.

Core Idea
Muwalat is sunnah: washing the next limb before the first dries is recommended, not required.

Many students assume that wudu must be performed without any interruption. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: while continuity (muwalat) is a recommended practice, it is not obligatory. We will examine the definition, the conditions that affect drying time, and the evidence from the Prophet's ﷺ consistent practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that muwalat in wudu is sunnah (يُسَنّ). Confirm that the ruling is sunnah, not fard or wajib.
Sunnah here means recommended; omission is not sinful but contrary to the preferred practice.
2
Define the term muwalat
The text gives two definitions: (1) washing the second limb before the first limb dries, under moderate weather and bodily conditions; (2) not engaging in another action between limbs without an excuse.
The second definition is an alternative opinion (قيل).
3
Analyze the conditions 'moderate time' and 'moderate body'
The passage explains that extreme heat, cold wind, or fever accelerate drying, so the definition adapts to normal circumstances. This shows that muwalat is relative.
The footnote elaborates on these conditions from Sharh Hidayat Ibn al-'Imad.
4
Examine the exception for excuses
If water runs out, the vessel overturns, or similar interruptions occur, the continuity is broken but there is no harm (لا بأس به) because the Prophet ﷺ sometimes experienced such delays.
This exception indicates that muwalat is not a strict obligation.
5
Identify the proof and its legal implication
The proof is the Prophet's persistent practice (مواظبة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم). In Hanafi usul, consistent action (fi'l) without explicit command indicates sunnah, not fard.
Compare with other schools that consider muwalat wajib; the Hanafi view relies on the absence of a decisive command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) يسن (الولاء) - بكسر الواو - المتابعة، وهو: أن يغسل العضو الثاني قبل جفاف الأول في زمان معتدل وبدن معتدل )، وقيل: أن لا يشتغل بينهما بعمل آخر بغير عذر، بأن فرغ الماء، أو انقلب الإناء، فذهب لطلب غيره، وما شبهه. فإن كان لا بأس به لمواظبة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم . (٤) قوله: (زمان معتدل) أي: أن لا يكون واقفاً في شدة الحر صيفاً ولا بشدة الريح شتاء فإن الجفاف يسارع فيهما، وقوله: (بدن معتدل) أي: أن لا يكون مصاب بالحمى فيسارع الجفاف إليه لأجل الحمى شرح هدية ابن العماد (۱۱۹) بتصرف.
(And) it is sunnah (continuity) - with a kasra on the waw - meaning consecutive sequence, and it is: that he washes the second limb before the first dries in moderate time and moderate body, and it is said: that he does not occupy himself between them with another action without an excuse, such as if the water runs out or the vessel overturns, and he goes to seek other water and the like. Then there is no harm in it due to the Prophet's persistence. (4) His saying: (moderate time) meaning that he should not be standing in extreme heat in summer nor in severe wind in winter, because drying is hastened in both; and his saying: (moderate body) meaning that he should not be afflicted with fever, because drying is hastened due to fever. From Sharh Hidayat Ibn al-'Imad (119) with adaptation.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah act of muwalat (continuity) in wudu. The author defines it as washing the next limb before the first dries, considering normal conditions of time and body. There is also an opinion that it means not engaging in any other action between limbs without a valid excuse, like fetching water if it runs out, which is permissible because the Prophet sometimes did so. The footnote clarifies the terms: moderate time excludes extreme hot or cold weather that accelerates drying, and moderate body excludes someone with fever. Thus, muwalat is a recommended practice, not obligatory.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-muwalat): The sequential continuity of washing limbs in wudu without significant delay.
المتابعة (al-mutaba'ah): Synonym for muwalat, meaning consecutive sequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Washing each limb three times.
B) Washing the next limb before the first dries under normal conditions.
C) Reciting du'a after wudu.
D) Wiping the head.

Key Takeaway
Muwalat (continuity) in wudu is a sunnah act, not fard; breaking it without excuse is disliked but the wudu remains valid.
